## 25 

2.2 Invasion of a CI capturing both ecological alleles and a mating-bias allele 

Next, our study examined the invasion of a CI mutant that captures locally adaptive ecological alleles together with a mating-bias allele. We first analyzed the case in which the CI captures all of the locally adaptive alleles, followed by the case in which it captures only a partial subset of these ecological alleles. 

The invasion dynamics of a CI that captures all locally adaptive ecological alleles and a mating-bias allele depend on the relative sizes of niches 𝐴𝐴 and 𝐵𝐵 .Fig 26 shows the post-CI invasion dynamics of a CI mutant that captures all locally adaptive ecological alleles together with the prevalent mating-bias allele in niche 𝐴𝐴 (CI = 𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 𝑋𝑋 ) in a system with three ecological loci. Under the 80% Habitat Resource Distribution 

setting ( 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴 : 𝐻𝐻 𝑁𝑁𝑏𝑏𝑃𝑃𝐼𝐼 𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃 : 𝑁𝑁 𝐵𝐵  = 0.4: 0.2: 0.4 ), the inva-sion of the CI mutant destroys the pre-established premating RI from the pre-CI phase. As a result, the 

𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  phase portrait becomes divergent, and the 𝑌𝑌 

mating-bias allele is eliminated. 

the same outcome is observed when niche 𝐴𝐴 is larger than niche 𝐵𝐵 . As illustrated in Fig 28, when the habitat resource distribution shown in Fig 27 is modified so that 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  > 𝑁𝑁 𝐵𝐵  (𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴 : 𝐻𝐻 𝑁𝑁𝑏𝑏𝑃𝑃𝐼𝐼 𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃 : 𝑁𝑁 𝐵𝐵  =0.6: 0.2: 0.2 ), the invasion of the CI mutant ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 =

𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 𝑋𝑋 ) likewise destroys the pre-established premating RI and renders the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  phase portrait divergent. 

In contrast, when niche 𝐵𝐵 is larger than niche 𝐴𝐴 , the outcome is different, and the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  phase portrait remains convergent. As shown in Fig 29a, when 𝑁𝑁 𝐵𝐵  >

𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  (𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴 : 𝐻𝐻 𝑁𝑁𝑏𝑏𝑃𝑃𝐼𝐼 𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃 : 𝑁𝑁 𝐵𝐵  = 0.1: 0.2: 0.7 ), the CI mutant capturing all ecological alleles ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 𝑋𝑋 ) is able to invade and shift the pre-CI 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  fixed point toward the lower-right corner of the phase portrait. The post-CI genotype distribution in Fig 29b shows that the 𝑋𝑋 

allele is able to hitchhike with the ecologically adaptive CI and rise to near fixation in niche 𝐴𝐴 .However, the CI invasion also increases the 𝑋𝑋 allele ratio in niche B, resulting in little net change in premating RI, as reflected by nearly unchanged pre-and post-CI 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 values. The post-CI 𝐻𝐻𝐻𝐻 decreases because fewer hybrid offspring are produced through inter-niche heterokaryotype matings. Nonetheless, when 𝛼𝛼 exceeds 3.1 in Fig 29a, the weakened mating bias leads to more inter-niche mating, which then gives the 𝑋𝑋 allele in niche 𝐵𝐵 a sufficient fitness advantage to eliminate the 𝑌𝑌 allele and causes the system to become divergent. 

When the CI captures only a partial subset of locally adaptive ecological alleles together with the prevalent mating-bias allele, the relative sizes of niches 𝐴𝐴 and 𝐵𝐵 do not appear to influence the outcome. In such cases, the system remains convergent following CI invasion. This is demonstrated in the examples shown in Figs 30a and 30b. In both scenarios (𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  > 𝑁𝑁 𝐵𝐵  and 𝑁𝑁 𝐵𝐵  > 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴 ), a CI mutant capturing two of the three locally adaptive ecological alleles together with the prevalent mating-bias allele ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 0𝑋𝑋 ) is able to invade the pre-established 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  barrier system, shift the pre-CI fixed point toward the lower-right corner of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  phase portrait, and strengthen the existing premating RI. 

In Figs 31a and 31b, the CI mutant captures only one of the three locally adaptive ecological alleles together with the prevalent mating-bias allele ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 =

𝑎𝑎 00 𝑋𝑋 ) in a pre-established 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  system with a fixed-point polymorphism. Although capturing only a single adaptive ecological allele does not by itself provide a fitness advantage over noninverted same-niche counterparts (see Fig 25), linkage with the locally prevalent mating-bias allele ( 𝑋𝑋 ) anchors the mating-bias allele in niche A. This coupling generates a synergistic invasion fitness advantage, enabling the CI to invade and reach fixation in niche 𝐴𝐴 , thereby strengthening the pre-existing premating RI. 

In our simulations, the CI mutant ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎 00 𝑋𝑋 ) can invade even when 𝛼𝛼 is as high as 0.999 to establish an 

𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  fixed point at 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  = 1/0 and generate premating RI. Thus, by varying 𝛼𝛼 between 0 and 1 (0 < 𝛼𝛼 < 1 ), such a CI—which captures one ecological allele together with a high mating-bias allele—can always invade to produce any level of premating RI from 0% to 100% between niche ecotypes. This result appears to hold in systems with any number of ecological gene loci greater than one. 

As demonstrated in Fig 31c, compared to systems in which the CI captures more than one adaptive ecological allele, a CI capturing only a single adaptive allele does not diminish disruptive ecological selection. Consequently, it more effectively drives the linked mating-bias allele toward fixation and tends to generate stronger premating RI, despite producing a higher proportion of hybrid offspring due to capturing fewer adaptive ecological alleles. 

The high invasion fitness of a CI that couples a single locally adaptive ecological allele with a mating-bias allele may enable it to invade a divergent 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁 

system and generate premating RI. This is demon-strated by the example in Fig 31d. On their own, a CI capturing only one locally adaptive ecological allele (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎 00 ) and a weak mating-bias allele X ( 𝛼𝛼 = 0.6 )cannot invade a divergent 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  phase portrait. However, when these alleles are linked within a CI (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎 00 𝑋𝑋 ), the CI can function as a magic trait that acquires synergistic fitness advantage over its same-niche ecotypes lacking the inversion. Consequently, 

> .CC-BY-NC-ND 4.0 International license available under a was not certified by peer review) is the author/funder, who has granted bioRxiv a license to display the preprint in perpetuity. It is made The copyright holder for this preprint (which this version posted April 4, 2026. ;https://doi.org/10.64898/2026.04.02.716010 doi: bioRxiv preprint

## 26 

the CI is able to invade successfully, leading to fixed-point polymorphism in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  phase portrait and premating RI. 

In Fig 32, a CI mutant with magic-trait properties that captures more than one locally adaptive ecological allele ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 0𝑋𝑋 ) exhibits greater invasion fitness than a CI that captures only a single adaptive allele (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎 00 𝑋𝑋 ). This is reflected by the shorter generation time ( 𝐶𝐶 95 ) required for the CI to reach its steady-state frequency. Capturing a larger number of locally adaptive ecological alleles provides the CI with a greater fitness advantage over noninverted same-niche ecotypes, thereby accelerating its invasion to near fixation in niche 𝐴𝐴 .

As illustrated by the example in Fig 31c, a CI that captures more locally adaptive alleles acquires higher invasion fitness; however, it also tends to produce weaker RI (reflected in higher 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 ). This general pattern is likewise confirmed in our simulations of a system with four ecological gene loci: a CI capturing more locally adaptive alleles together with the locally favored mating-bias allele invades more rapidly due to its enhanced invasion fitness, yet tends to reach a lower equilibrium frequency and to produce weaker RI (higher 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 ) along with reduced residual hybrid loss (lower 𝐻𝐻𝐻𝐻 ). 

Conversely, if the CI captures a locally maladap-tive ecological allele, its fitness is reduced relative to same-niche ecotypes without the inversion. This outcome is shown in Fig 33, where a CI arising in niche 𝐴𝐴 but capturing a maladaptive allele originating from niche 𝐵𝐵 (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎 0𝑏𝑏 𝑋𝑋 ) exhibits lower fitness than its noninverted counterparts in niche 𝐴𝐴 and cannot invade. 

With 40% hybrid habitat resources (Fig 34b), the results shown in Figs 34a and 34c demonstrate that a mutant CI capturing a partial set of locally adaptive ecological alleles together with the locally prevalent mating-bias allele ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 0𝑋𝑋 ) has greater invasion fitness than a CI capturing the same adaptive ecological alleles without the mating-bias allele ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 =

𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 0). This difference is reflected by the shorter invasion time (𝐶𝐶 95 ) and the final steady-state frequency of the CI capturing both allele types. These outcomes imply the existence of positive selection favoring the linkage of locally adaptive ecological and mating-bias alleles within a low-recombination region of the genome. 

Another key observation from the CI invasion plots in Figs 34a and 34c and the post-CI genotype distribution shown in Fig 34d is that, when hybrid habitat resources support the existence of viable ecological hybrids, the invading CIs tend to persist in a polymorphism with uninverted ecotypes in their adaptive niche (niche 𝐴𝐴 ). This occurs because hybrid matings can continually regenerate the uninverted parental ecotypes. As a result, a balance between im-migration and selection maintains the polymorphism between inverted and uninverted genotypes in the parental niche and prevents the CIs from reaching fixation. 

Fig 34e shows that when an ecologically adaptive CI mutant captures the wrong mating-bias allele, it suffers reduced fitness and cannot invade. In this example, the CI mutant ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 0𝑌𝑌 ) captures the prevalent mating-bias allele ( 𝑌𝑌 ) in niche 𝐵𝐵 instead of the prevalent allele ( 𝑋𝑋 ) in niche A, after nonrandom assortment of mating-bias alleles across niches has already been established. This mismatch reduces the CI’s fitness and prevents its invasion. 

Lastly, the examples in Figs 35a–35c illustrate that when large populations of viable ecological hybrids are present, hybrid matings can regenerate substantial numbers of uninverted parental ecotypes. Conse-quently, a large hybrid swarm may overwhelm and ultimately eliminate the CI population from the polymorphism between inverted and uninverted genotypes in the parental niche. 

## II. SYSTEMS WITH MULTILOCUS MATING -BIAS GENOTYPES 

The GUI application MultiSexCI was used to examine how incorporating an arbitrary number of mating-bias gene loci influences system dynamics. To focus solely on pre-CI dynamics, the post-CI phase of the simulation was disabled by setting the number of post-CI generations (𝑃𝑃 𝑜𝑜 𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃 -𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 𝐺𝐺 𝑃𝑃𝑛𝑛 ) to zero. In subsequent simulations, the post-CI phase was enabled, and CI mutations capturing different combinations of mating-bias alleles were introduced to investigate their invasion dynamics and effects on overall system behavior. 

## 1 The effects of having multilocus mating-bias genotypes (without CI invasion) 

We first examined how varying the number of 

mating‐bias gene loci affects population dynamics in 

the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait. To do so, the post-CI phase was disabled by setting 𝑃𝑃 𝑜𝑜 𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃 -𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 𝐺𝐺 = 0 . Fig 36a shows the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase-portrait solution of the mathemati-cal model in Fig 3, where the mating-bias genotype is determined by a single gene locus and ecological selection is modeled by a two-niche system without viable hybrids. The strength of disruptive ecological selection is specified by the offspring return ratio 𝑓𝑓 ,which ranges from 0 to 0.5, with lower values indicating stronger ecological selection. As shown in 

> .CC-BY-NC-ND 4.0 International license available under a was not certified by peer review) is the author/funder, who has granted bioRxiv a license to display the preprint in perpetuity. It is made The copyright holder for this preprint (which this version posted April 4, 2026. ;https://doi.org/10.64898/2026.04.02.716010 doi: bioRxiv preprint

## 27 

Fig 36a, under the specified parametric conditions, the 

𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait converges to a fixed point, thereby producing premating RI. 

In Figs 36b–36d, we incrementally increase the number of mating-bias gene loci from one to four by adjusting the number of allelic positions in the CI Vector input, while keeping all other parametric values identical to those in Fig 36a. The results show that as the number of mating-bias loci increases from one to two and then three, the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  fixed point progressively shifts away from the lower-right corner of the phase portrait. This shift is accompanied by increased pre-CI 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 and 𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃  values at the fixed points, indicating weakened premating RI as the number of loci increases. With two mating-bias loci, the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋 

phase portrait in Fig 36b remains globally convergent, but when the number of loci is increased to three, an invasion-resistant pattern emerges in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋 

phase portrait in Fig 36c, which prevents invasion by small mutant populations near the origin. Further increasing the number of mating-bias loci to four in Fig 36d causes the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait to become globally divergent, with no fixed point and no premating RI. 

It should be noted that the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait only tracks changes in the population ratios of the extreme parental mating-bias genotypes (e.g.,  𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  and 

𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 , or  𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  and  𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ). To determine whether a fixed point may exist at hybrid genotypes elsewhere in the higher-dimensional genotype space beyond the 

𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  dimension, the post-CI genotype distribution must be examined using the Post-CI Distribution tab in the interface. Across all simulation runs, the results consistently indicate that at most one fixed point— represented by a single peak in the genotype distribution—exists within the full high-dimensional phase portrait defined by the number of mating-bias loci. 

In Figs 36e–36g, we investigate how varying the maximum mating-bias value 𝛼𝛼 in the Matching Compatibility Table (Fig 15) affects system dynamics. As shown in Fig 36e, reducing 𝛼𝛼 from 0.3 to 0.2 in a system with three mating-bias loci increases the mating bias between the extreme genotypes, facilitates system convergence, and shifts the fixed point closer to the lower-right corner of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  phase portrait, resulting in stronger premating RI. This reduction in 𝛼𝛼 

also removes the invasion-resistance pattern that the 

𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait in Fig 36c exhibits when 𝛼𝛼 =0.3 .

A comparison of the pre-CI genotype distributions in Fig 36f, where 𝛼𝛼 = 0.2 , and Fig 18b, where 𝛼𝛼 =0.3 , shows that as 𝛼𝛼 decreases, the genotype distribu-tion shifts toward the extreme ends and fewer intermediate hybrids are produced. Correspondingly, both 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 and 𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃  values decline, indicating stronger RI. In general, as the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  fixed point shifts closer to the lower-right corner of the phase portrait—reflecting increased premating RI—the genotype distribution becomes more concentrated at the extremes and leaves fewer intermediate hybrids. 

When 𝛼𝛼 is reduced to zero, no mating-bias hybrid offspring are produced. As shown in Fig 36g, only the extreme mating-bias genotypes ( 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  and  𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) remain at the fixed point. Both 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 and 𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃  are zero, and premating RI is complete. 

In Fig 36h, strengthening disruptive ecological selection by reducing the value of 𝑓𝑓 in Fig 36c from 0.125 to 0.1 produces the same effect as reducing the mating-bias parameter 𝛼𝛼 . This reduction in 𝑓𝑓 also removes the invasion-resistance pattern observed in Fig 36c and shifts the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  fixed point closer to the lower-right corner of the phase portrait. 

Fig 37a demonstrates that increasing the number of matching rounds ( 𝑛𝑛 ) can reduce the invasion-resistant region in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait in systems with multiple mating-bias loci, just as in the case of systems with a single mating-bias locus. 

Similarly, as illustrated in Fig 37b, reducing the niche size 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  may open a wedge-shaped region along the x-axis within the invasion-resistant pattern, which enables invasion by extreme-𝑋𝑋 (𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 ) mutant popula-tions. However, there appears to be a lower threshold of 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  below which the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait becomes divergent, as well as an upper threshold above which divergence also occurs. These effects arise symmetrically around 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  = 0.5 . More generally, simulations with different numbers of mating-bias loci also show that 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  = 0.5 yields the greatest reduction in the invasion-resistant region, and outside a symmetric range around this value, the system becomes divergent. 

## 2 Invasion of a CI capturing mating-bias alleles in a two-niche system with multilocus mating-bias genotypes 

The invasion dynamics of a CI capturing mating-bias alleles in the multilocus mating-bias model are examined by specifying the CI Vector and setting the Post-CI G to be greater than zero. 

The example in Fig 38 shows that when 𝛼𝛼 = 0 in a system with three mating-bias gene loci, no mating-bias hybrids are produced in the pre-CI phase. Under these conditions, a CI capturing mating-bias alleles cannot invade because the invasion fitness of such a CI depends on its ability to reduce the effective number of mating-bias loci and suppress the production of intermediate mating-bias hybrids,

It is made The copyright holder for this preprint (which this version posted April 4, 2026. ;https://doi.org/10.64898/2026.04.02.716010 doi: bioRxiv preprint

## 28 

thereby enhancing premating RI and reducing maladaptive ecological hybrid loss. When no intermediate mating-bias hybrids are present to be eliminated, there is no selective advantage for the CI to invade. 

In Fig 39a, increasing the value of 𝛼𝛼 from 0 to 0.3 in Fig 38 restores ecological hybrid loss due to disruptive ecological selection. Under the same parametric conditions as in Fig 36c, the pre-CI 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋 

phase portrait again displays an invasion-resistant pattern that prevents invasion by small extreme-𝑋𝑋 

mutant populations near the origin. However, in the presence of hybrid loss, a CI mutant capturing all extreme-𝑋𝑋 mating-bias alleles (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) gains enhanced invasion fitness, as genotypes carrying the CI experience reduced ecological hybrid offspring loss in inter-niche matings compared to same-niche counterparts without the inversion. This fitness advantage enables the CI to overcome the invasion-resistant pattern and successfully invade, resulting in a post-CI 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  fixed point that lies closer to the lower-right corner of the phase portrait and yields stronger premating RI. As shown in Fig 39b, the post-CI genotype distribution consists exclusively of extreme mating-bias genotypes, with no intermediate hybrids. This outcome demonstrates that, by reducing the effective number of mating-bias gene loci from three to one, the CI is able to eliminate the production of mating-bias hybrids and facilitate system convergence. 

The Initial Genotype Distribution Table in Fig 40 illustrates the scheme used to specify the initial extreme-𝑋𝑋 mutant population from which a CI mutant arises. This small population provides the  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles at each mating-bias locus for the CI to capture. In general, the extreme-𝑋𝑋 population is set to a ratio of 0.01, and the remainder of the population consists solely of the extreme-𝑌𝑌 genotype ( 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋  = 0.01 , 𝐴𝐴 𝑌𝑌  =0.99 , 𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  = 0 , 𝐵𝐵 𝑌𝑌  = 1 ). 

Fig 41a shows that a CI mutant capturing three of four x-type mating-bias alleles (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 0) and arising in niche 𝐴𝐴 is able to invade a divergent 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋 

system that has no pre-CI fixed point and is composed entirely of the extreme-𝑌𝑌 genotype. However, as shown in Fig 41b, the CI does not become fixed at the extreme-𝑋𝑋 genotype ( 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ). Instead, it ultimately fixes at the hybrid mating-bias genotype 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 .

A likely explanation is that when the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  mutant first arises in niche 𝐴𝐴 , which is initially composed entirely of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  genotype, CI introgression through intra-niche matings rapidly generates asubstantial 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  population. The low mating bias between 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  and 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  increases intra-niche matings and reduces inter-niche matings for 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 .At 𝛼𝛼 = 0.3 , the mating bias favoring 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  in inter-niche matings is too weak to offset its low frequency, allowing the much larger 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  population to gain greater fitness from more inter-niche matings despite its weaker inter-niche mating bias. Consequently, 

𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  eliminates 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  early in the invasion, before 

𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  can increase in size. 

However, when the maximum mating bias between the extreme genotypes is strengthened, the outcome changes. As shown in Fig 41c, reducing 𝛼𝛼 from 0.3 to 0.15 strengthens the mating bias between 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  and 

𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 , restoring the inter-niche mating advantage of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  genotype. This enhanced advantage enables the extreme-𝑋𝑋 genotype ( 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) to become predominant in niche 𝐴𝐴 following CI invasion, resulting in stronger premating RI. 

Similar invasion dynamics are observed when the invading CI mutant captures only two of the four extreme-𝑋𝑋 mating-bias alleles ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 00 ). Under the same parametric conditions as in Fig 41a, this CI (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 00 ) is unable to invade the divergent 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋 

system when 𝛼𝛼 = 0.3 . However, as shown in Fig 41d, reducing 𝛼𝛼 from 0.3 to 0.2 allows the CI to invade and eventually become fixed at the hybrid genotype 

𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 . Further reducing 𝛼𝛼 to 0.1 in Fig 41e enables the extreme-X genotype (𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) to become predominant in niche 𝐴𝐴 following CI invasion, resulting in stronger premating RI. 

In general, a CI mutant that captures only a single mating-bias allele gains no fitness advantage over its same-niche counterparts without the inversion and cannot invade. This is because, to benefit from suppressing the breakdown of favorable allele combinations, a CI must capture more than one advantageous allele to preserve their linkage and epistatic effects. This outcome is demonstrated in Fig 42a, where a CI mutant capturing only one of three  𝑁𝑁 -type mating-bias alleles ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑋𝑋 00 ) in a three-locus system fails to invade. Consequently, as shown in Fig 42b, the post-CI genotype distribution remains unchanged from the pre-CI phase, and the post-CI 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 

and 𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃  values are identical to their pre-CI values. 

In Fig 42c, when the CI captures two of the three extreme-𝑋𝑋 mating-bias alleles ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 0), recombi-nation suppression becomes effective, allowing the CI to invade successfully and establish stronger premating RI. The post-CI genotype distribution shown in Fig 42d reveals increased nonrandom assortment of mating-bias alleles across the two niches and a reduction in mating-bias hybrids compared to the distribution in Fig 42b. The CI becomes fixed in niche 𝐴𝐴 at mating-bias genotypes closer to the extreme end, with the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  genotype becoming predominant. Following CI invasion, both post-CI 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 and 𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃  values decrease relative to their pre-CI values, indicating strengthened premating RI.

Fig 42e shows the post-CI genotype distribution following the invasion of a CI that captures all three extreme-𝑋𝑋 mating-bias alleles ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ). Invasion of the CI effectively reduces the number of mating-bias gene loci experienced by the system from three to one and yields a post-CI distribution composed exclu-sively of extreme genotypes, with no intermediate hybrids. Consequently, the reduction in post-CI 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 

and 𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃  values is maximal. 

Compared to the case where the CI captures only two alleles ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 0), capturing all three alleles (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) further increases the CI’s invasion fitness, allowing it to reach a higher steady-state frequency in niche 𝐴𝐴 and to do so more rapidly. This is evidenced by a lower  𝐶𝐶 95 value, defined as the number of generations required for the CI to reach 95% of its final steady-state frequency. These results demonstrate that a CI capturing a greater number of favorable mating-bias alleles possesses greater invasion fitness. 

Figs 43a and 43b show a system with four‐locus mating‐bias genotypes under parametric conditions 

that allow system convergence. In Fig 43a, during the pre-CI phase, a small extreme-𝑋𝑋 mutant population (𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋  = 0.01 ) is able to invade a population composed entirely of extreme-𝑌𝑌 genotypes, resulting in a fixed-point polymorphism in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait and the establishment of premating RI. Subsequently, in the post-CI phase, a CI mutant capturing all extreme-

𝑋𝑋 mating-bias alleles ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) is able to invade, shifting the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  fixed point further toward the lower-right corner of the phase portrait and thereby producing stronger premating RI. 

In Fig 43b, the pre-CI phase is omitted by setting 

𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃 -𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 𝐺𝐺 = 1 . Under these conditions, the CI mutant (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 , 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 = 0.01 ) is able to invade directly into a population consisting solely of the extreme-𝑌𝑌 

genotype. The 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait converges to the same fixed-point solution as in Fig 43a and produces the same level of premating RI. 

Fig 43c shows the post-CI genotype distribution following CI invasion ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 , 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 = 0.01 ) in Figs 43a and 43b. In both cases, invasion of the CI reduces the effective number of mating-bias gene loci to one. 

In Fig 44a, increasing the value of 𝛼𝛼 from 0.2 to 0.25 causes the four-locus system shown in Fig 43a to become divergent. Under these conditions, the pre-CI 

𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait exhibits a globally divergent pattern that prevents invasion by a high-mating-bias mutant ( 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 ) with an initial population ratio of 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋  =0.01 .

Nevertheless, as demonstrated in Fig 44b, a CI mutant capturing all extreme-𝑋𝑋 mating-bias alleles (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 , 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 = 0.01 ) can still invade this diver-gent system and establish an 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  fixed-point polymorphism and premating RI. This occurs because the CI is able to reduce the effective number of mating-bias gene loci experienced by the system from four to one, thereby restoring convergence. As a result, the post-CI genotype distribution shown in Fig 44c consists exclusively of the extreme mating-bias genotypes ( 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  and  𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ), with no intermediate hybrids. 

A further observation is that the range of initial 

𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  population ratios from which the CI mutant (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) can invade appears to be confined to a wedge-shaped region near the base of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋 

phase portrait. This region can be visualized by selecting the Hide Divergent Ax/Bx Lines option in the interface, which removes trajectories that terminate on the diagonal line and displays only the non-divergent portion of the phase portrait. 

Figs 44d–44g examine how invasion dynamics change as the CI mutant in Fig 44b captures progressively fewer extreme-𝑋𝑋 mating-bias alleles. In Fig 44d, the CI captures three of four  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 0), and its invasion reduces the effective number of mating-bias loci experienced by the system from four to two. Accordingly, the post-CI genotype distribution in Fig 44e resembles that of a system with only two mating-bias loci. Similarly, in Fig 44f, when the CI captures two alleles ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 00 ), the effective number of loci is reduced from four to three, and the post-CI genotype distribution in Fig 44g contains a total of eight genotypes at the fixed point, consistent with a three-locus system. The phase portraits in Figs 44d and 44f also show that capturing fewer mating-bias alleles causes the wedge-shaped region permitting CI invasion to contract progressively toward the  𝑁𝑁 -axis. This region disappears entirely when the CI captures only a single  𝑁𝑁  allele ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁 000 ), which offers no fitness advantage for the CI to invade (see Fig 42a). 

Figs 44h and 44i show how the phase‐portrait 

dynamics change when the initial 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  population ratios fall outside the wedge-shaped region in Fig 44f that permits invasion by the CI mutant ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 00 ). 

As illustrated by the phase‐portrait trajectory in Fig 

44h, the higher mating bias of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋  population initially allows it to increase during the early post-CI phase. However, once the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋  ratio exceeds approximately 0.6, the increased production of intermediate hybrids shifts the genotype distribution toward genotypes carrying more  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles, which begin to displace those carrying more  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles. As a result, the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  population vector turns toward the diagonal line in the phase portrait and gradually converges toward the origin. Over time, the post-CI genotype distribution shown in Fig 44i becomes

increasingly skewed in favor of genotypes containing more  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles. Ultimately, all genotypes carrying 

𝑁𝑁 -type alleles are eliminated, and the population becomes fixed for the extreme-𝑌𝑌 genotype. 

So far, Figs 44a–44i examine the invasion of a mutant CI capturing mating-bias alleles arising from a small extreme-𝑋𝑋 population ( 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋  = 0.01 ) near the origin and invading a divergent system composed entirely of extreme-𝑌𝑌 genotypes. In Figs 45a–45d, the CI instead originates directly from the origin ( 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /

𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  = 0/0 ). Because no  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles exist at the uncaptured loci of the CI vector, those positions are automatically occupied by  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles. As a result, only two mating-bias genotypes are possible in the system, and CI invasion invariably reduces the effective number of mating-bias loci to one. 

In Fig 45a, invasion of a CI mutant ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 0)arising from the origin into a system with four mating-bias loci and initially fixed for the extreme-𝑌𝑌 genotype produces a post-CI distribution containing only  𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 

and  𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  genotypes, with no intermediate hybrids between them. This outcome is equivalent to that of a system with a single mating-bias locus. A similar outcome occurs in Fig 45b, where the CI mutant ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 =

𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 00 ) is functionally equivalent to 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 . Again, only two genotypes ( 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  and  𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) remain after invasion, with no intermediate hybrids, and the effective number of mating-bias loci is reduced to one. In both cases (Figs 45a and 45b), reducing the effective number of loci to one eliminates intermediate hybrid formation and promotes CI invasion and convergence. 

However, the premating RI generated by such invasions tends to be weaker compared to cases where the CI captures all extreme-𝑋𝑋 alleles. This is because the mating bias between the post-CI hybrid genotype and the extreme-𝑌𝑌 genotype (e.g.,  𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  vs  𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  or 

𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  vs  𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) is lower than 𝛼𝛼 , the maximum mating bias between the extreme-𝑋𝑋 and extreme-𝑌𝑌 genotypes (𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  and  𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ). Therefore, CI mutants that capture only a partial set of extreme-𝑋𝑋 alleles generate weaker RI than CI mutants that capture all extreme-𝑋𝑋 alleles. 

The examples in Figs 45c and 45d show that a CI mutant ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 00 ) arising from the origin can successfully invade a divergent system with six mating-bias loci and establish an 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  fixed-point polymorphism and premating RI. Ordinarily, systems with such a high number of mating-bias loci are highly resistant to invasion by alternative high-mating-bias genotypes without the invasion advantage conferred by a CI. As shown in Fig 45c, a narrow region near the 

𝑁𝑁 -axis in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait permits invasion by the CI arising from the origin. Following invasion, the post-CI genotype distribution in Fig 45d indicates that the effective number of mating-bias loci experienced by the system is reduced from six to one. However, because the mating bias between the CI genotype and the extreme-𝑌𝑌 genotype depends on the number of  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles captured by the CI, the resulting premating RI is weaker when fewer  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles are captured. The greater the number of  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles captured by the CI, the stronger the effective mating bias and the resulting RI. 

For comparison, Fig 45e shows the post-CI genotype distribution of the same CI mutant ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 =

𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 00 ) when it arises from a small initial extreme-

𝑋𝑋 population ( 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  = 0.01 ), rather than from the origin. In this case, the effective number of mating-bias loci is reduced from six to three instead of one. As demonstrated by the presence of a restricted invasion zone in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait in Fig 45c and by the differing post-CI distribution in Fig 45e, the final invasion outcome of a CI mutant in a divergent system is highly sensitive to the number, location, and initial frequency of  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles accompanying the CI at its inception.

Fig 36c. In a two-niche model without viable ecological hybrids, having three gene loci for mating-bias genotypes causes an invasion-resistant pattern to emerge in the phase portrait. Fig 36d. In a two-niche model without viable ecological hybrids, having four gene loci for mating-bias genotypes causes the phase portrait to become divergent. Increasing the number of mating-bias loci in Fig 36c from three to four (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 0000 ) results in a divergent 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait with no fixed points and no premating RI between the niche-

𝐴𝐴 and niche-𝐵𝐵 ecotypes.

Fig 36e. In a two-niche model without viable ecological hybrids and with three gene loci for mating-bias genotypes, increasing the maximum mating bias produces stronger premating RI. Reducing 𝛼𝛼 from 0.3 to 0.2 in the three-locus case shown in Fig 36c eliminates the invasion-resistant pattern in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait. With the stronger mating bias, the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  fixed point shifts closer to the lower-right corner, and the fixed-point 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 /𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃  values decrease from 0.4384/0.3288 to 0.30598/0.22949 ,reflecting stronger premating RI. 

Fig 36f. Bar graph showing the fixed-point mating-bias genotype distribution in a system with three mating-bias loci. The Pre-CI Distribution bar graph displays the genotype distribution at the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  fixed point shown in Fig 36e. Because 𝛼𝛼 is nonzero, viable mating-bias hybrids are produced, but their distribution is skewed toward the like-kind parental genotypes (i.e., concentrated near the  𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  and 

𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  ends). This skew reflects nonrandom assortment of incompatible mating-bias genotypes from opposite ends of the Matching Compatibility Table (Fig 15), resulting in premating RI between niche ecotypes. 

Fig 36g. Bar graph showing that when 𝜶𝜶 = 𝒂𝒂 , no viable hybrids exist in the fixed-point mating-bias genotype distribution in a system with three mating-bias loci. 

When 𝛼𝛼 in Fig 36e is set to zero, the Pre-CI Distribution bar graph shows that all hybrid genotypes are eliminated, leaving only the extreme parental mating-bias genotypes at the resulting fixed point 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  = 1/0 .

from 0.125 to 0.1 in the three-locus case shown in Fig 36c eliminates the invasion-resistant pattern in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait. With stronger disruptive ecological selection (lower 

𝑓𝑓 ), the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  fixed point shifts closer to the lower-right corner, and the fixed-point 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 /𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃  values decrease from 

0.4384/0.3288 to 0.36796/0.29437 , reflecting stronger premating RI.

Fig 37a. Increasing the number of matching rounds ( 𝒏𝒏 )reduces the invasion-resistant region in the 𝑨𝑨 𝑿𝑿 /𝑩𝑩 𝑿𝑿 

phase portrait of a system with three mating-bias loci. 

When the number of matching rounds in Fig 36c is increased from 𝑛𝑛 = 4 to 𝑛𝑛 = 10 , while keeping all other parametric values unchanged, the invasion-resistant region in the 

𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait is reduced toward the origin. 

Fig 37b. Decreasing the size of niche 𝑨𝑨 (𝑵𝑵 𝑨𝑨 ) reduces the invasion-resistant region along the  𝑨𝑨 -axis in the 𝑨𝑨 𝑿𝑿 /𝑩𝑩 𝑿𝑿 

phase portrait of a system with three mating-bias loci. 

When the niche-𝐴𝐴 size in Fig 36c is reduced from 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  = 0.6 

to 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  = 0.4 , while keeping all other parametric values unchanged, the invasion-resistant region contracts away from the  𝑁𝑁 -axis. This creates a wedge-shaped area lying along the  𝑁𝑁 -axis and pointing toward the origin, within which invasion is possible for initial 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  population ratios falling inside this region. As 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  is further reduced toward 0.3, the wedge-shaped region becomes increasingly compressed toward the  𝑁𝑁 -axis, and the fixed point shifts closer to the  𝑁𝑁 -axis and farther from the lower-right corner of the phase portrait. When 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  ≤ 0.3 , the wedge-shaped region disappears entirely, and the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait becomes divergent. Similarly, increasing 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  greater than 0.5 produces a comparable effect along the  𝑁𝑁 -axis, and the phase portrait becomes globally divergent once 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  ≥ 0.7 .

Fig 38. Post-CI Distribution bar graph showing that an adaptive CI ( 𝑪𝑪𝑪𝑪 = 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨 ) cannot invade when 𝜶𝜶 = 𝒂𝒂  and no mating-bias hybrids exist in a system with three mating-bias loci. In Fig 36g, setting 𝛼𝛼 = 0 eliminates all mating-bias hybrids at the 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  fixed point. When 𝛼𝛼 = 0 ,an adaptive CI mutant ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) cannot invade because its invasion fitness depends on reducing the production of mating-bias hybrid offspring—by lowering the effective number of mating-bias loci experienced by the system—and thereby increasing premating RI to reduce maladaptive ecological hybrid loss. When 𝛼𝛼 = 0 and 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 /𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃  = 0/0 ,maximum mating bias has already been achieved, which leaves no room for a CI to further enhance it. 

Fig 39a. When ecological hybrid loss is present ( 𝜶𝜶 =

𝒂𝒂 . 𝟑𝟑 ), an adaptive CI mutant ( 𝑪𝑪𝑪𝑪 = 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨 ) can overcome the invasion-resistant pattern in the 𝑨𝑨 𝑿𝑿 /𝑩𝑩 𝑿𝑿  phase por-trait and invade to establish fixed-point polymorphism and premating RI. Increasing 𝛼𝛼 in Fig 38 from 0 to 0.3 restores ecological hybrid loss due to disruptive ecological selection, causing the pre-CI 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait to once again display the invasion-resistant pattern shown in Fig 36c. Under these conditions, hybrid loss allows a locally adaptive CI mutant ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 , 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 = 0.01 ), arising from a small extreme-𝑋𝑋 mutant population (𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁  = 0.01 , as specified in Fig 40), to overcome the invasion-resistant pattern and successfully invade. This results in a post-CI 

𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  fixed point that lies closer to the lower-right corner of the phase portrait and produces stronger premating RI.

Further decreasing 𝛼𝛼 in Fig 41d from 0.2 to 0.1 establishes the extreme-𝑋𝑋 genotype ( 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) as the predominant mating-bias genotype in niche A. 

Fig 42a. A CI mutant ( 𝑪𝑪𝑪𝑪 = 𝑨𝑨𝒂𝒂𝒂𝒂 ) capturing only a single mating-bias allele acquires no fitness advantage and cannot invade. Given the parametric values shown, the pre-CI 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  phase portrait is globally convergent with a fixed point at 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  = 0.9772/0.0430 in a system with three mating-bias loci. In the post-CI phase, a CI capturing only one locally prevalent mating-bias allele ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁 00 ) gains no fitness benefit, because preserving a favorable association of linked alleles requires capturing more than one allele. As a result, the CI has no invasion advantage and cannot invade.

Fig 42b. Post-CI Distribution bar graph showing the genotype distribution after a CI mutant (𝑪𝑪𝑪𝑪 = 𝑨𝑨𝒂𝒂𝒂𝒂 )capturing only a single locally favored mating-bias allele fails to invade. Fig 42c. A CI mutant ( 𝑪𝑪𝑪𝑪 = 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝒂𝒂 ) capturing two of the three locally prevalent mating-bias alleles in a conver-gent system with an 𝑨𝑨 𝑿𝑿 /𝑩𝑩 𝑿𝑿  fixed-point polymorphism gains a fitness advantage that allows it to invade and strengthen existing premating RI. Using the same parametric values as in Fig 42a, a CI mutant that captures two locally favored mating-bias alleles ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 0) benefits from the linkage among these alleles and gains an invasion fitness advantage. As the CI spreads through the niche-𝐴𝐴 

population, it produces stronger premating RI, as indicated by reduced post-CI 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 and 𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃  values. The time required to reach 95% of the steady-state 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 value (0.9504) is 488 generations ( 𝐶𝐶 95 = 488 ). 

Fig 42d. Post-CI Distribution bar graph showing the genotype distribution after a CI mutant (𝑪𝑪𝑪𝑪 = 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝒂𝒂 )invades and strengthens premating RI. The bar graph displays the genotype distribution following the CI invasion shown in Fig 42c. It reveals nonrandom assortment toward the extreme genotypes, with smaller hybrid populations persisting near those extremes. 

Fig 42e. Post-CI Distribution bar graph showing the genotype distribution after a CI mutant capturing all locally prevalent mating-bias alleles ( 𝑪𝑪𝑪𝑪 = 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨 ) invades and produces stronger premating RI. When the CI mutant in Fig 42c instead captures all three locally prevalent mating-bias alleles ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ), the post-CI genotype distribution shows that opposing mating-bias genotypes persist only at the extreme ends of the distribution, with all intermediate hybrids eliminated. The further reduction in post-CI 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 and 

𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃  values reflects the stronger premating RI achieved by this CI invasion. The time required to reach 95% of the steady-state 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 value (0.9646) is 310 generations ( 𝐶𝐶 95 = 310 ). Compared to the CI that captures only two adaptive mating-bias alleles ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 0), the CI capturing all three ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 =

𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) attains a higher steady-state 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 value in a shorter time, confirming that CI invasion fitness increases with the number of locally favorable mating-bias alleles captured.

Fig 44a. Weakening mating bias by increasing 𝜶𝜶

Fig 44b. A CI mutant ( 𝑪𝑪𝑪𝑪 = 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨 ) can invade a divergent system with four mating-bias loci to generate an 𝑨𝑨 𝑿𝑿 /𝑩𝑩 𝑿𝑿

Fig 44h. When the initial 𝑨𝑨 𝑿𝑿 /𝑩𝑩 𝑿𝑿  population ratios fall outside the region in the 𝑨𝑨 𝑿𝑿 /𝑩𝑩 𝑿𝑿  phase portrait that allows a CI mutant ( 𝑪𝑪𝑪𝑪 = 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝒂𝒂𝒂𝒂 ) to invade and converge to a fixed point, the CI ultimately fails to invade and all genotypes containing the  𝑨𝑨 -type alleles are eliminated. 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 00 , 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 = 0.01 ) arising within this initial 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋 

population. Consequently, the CI fails to invade, and the population vector is driven toward the diagonal line in the phase portrait, eventually collapsing toward the origin. 

Fig 44i. Post-CI genotype distribution graph showing genotypes closer to the extreme-𝒀𝒀 genotype eliminating those closer to the extreme-𝑿𝑿 genotype. The bar graph displays the post-CI genotype distribution from Fig 44h after 10,000 post-CI generations. The increasingly skewed distribution reflects the progressive elimination of genotypes carrying more  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles by those carrying more  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles. With the initial 𝐴𝐴 𝑋𝑋 /𝐵𝐵 𝑋𝑋  population ratios set to 0.3/0.1, as specified in the Initial Genotype Distribution Table on the right, a CI mutant ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 00 ) is unable to invade. Eventually, all genotypes containing  𝑁𝑁 -type alleles are driven to extinction, and the population becomes fixed for the extreme-𝑌𝑌 genotype.

(𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) and extreme-𝑌𝑌 (𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁𝑁 ) genotypes, the resulting post-CI 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 and 𝐻𝐻 𝑃𝑃  values are higher than those shown in Figs 44c and 44e, indicating weaker premating RI.

## III. S YSTEMS WITH MULTILOCUS ECOLOGICAL GENOTYPES AND SINGLE -LOCUS HYBRID -INCOMPATIBILITY GENOTYPES 

We first examine how population dynamics in the one-locus-equivalent model of hybrid incompatibility depend on the viability ratio ( 𝑣𝑣 ) and the strength of ecological selection ( 𝑓𝑓 ) in the absence of premating barriers ( 𝛼𝛼 = 1 ). We then introduce premating barriers to assess how mating bias (𝛼𝛼 < 1 ) modifies an established postzygotic barrier. 

We next adapt the MultiEcoCI application to examine how incorporating multiple ecological loci and viable hybrid populations alters system dynamics. This is done by replacing the mating-bias parameter (𝛼𝛼 ) with the viability ratio ( 𝑣𝑣 ) and fixing the number of matching rounds ( 𝑛𝑛 ) to one, as described in the Methodology section. Pre-CI dynamics are examined by setting the number of post-CI generations to zero. Subsequently, the post-CI phase is enabled and CI mutations capturing different combinations of ecological alleles are introduced to evaluate their invasion dynamics and effects on system behavior. 

Finally, we use the MultiCompCI application to investigate how having multiple loci underlying hybrid incompatibility affects system dynamics and how the invasion of CIs capturing various extreme-type incompatibility alleles shapes the evolutionary outcomes of the system. 

## 1 Interaction dynamics of ecological, mating-bias, and hybrid-incompatibility alleles in single-locus-equivalent models 

By replacing 𝛼𝛼 with 𝑣𝑣 , mating-bias alleles 𝑋𝑋 and 𝑌𝑌 

with incompatibility alleles 𝑃𝑃 and 𝑄𝑄 , and setting 𝑛𝑛 =1, the GUI program in Fig 7—originally used to simulate the population dynamics of the mathematical model in Fig 3—can also be used to compute the population dynamics of a one-locus-equivalent model of hybrid incompatibility in the absence of premating barriers (see Methodology). 

As shown in Fig 46a, when 𝑓𝑓 = 0.5 , disruptive ecological selection is absent and all individuals encounter one another randomly and mate freely in a single panmictic population. Under these conditions, the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  phase portrait is divergent for all values of 

𝑣𝑣 . Provided that 𝑣𝑣 < 1 , the more prevalent hybrid incompatibility allele ( 𝑃𝑃 or 𝑄𝑄 ) eliminates the less prevalent allele in the population through attritive interactions. In Fig 46b, when 𝑓𝑓 < 0.5 , weak disruptive ecological selection (i.e., relatively large 𝑓𝑓 )combined with weak incompatibility selection (large 

𝑣𝑣 ) still produces a divergent 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  phase portrait. 

In contrast, in Fig 46c, when disruptive ecological selection is complete ( 𝑓𝑓 = 0 ), no viable offspring (hybrid or parental) are produced from inter-niche matings, and no gene flow occurs between niche populations. Consequently, the more prevalent incompatibility allele eliminates the less prevalent allele within each niche for all values of 𝑣𝑣 in the range 

0 ≤ 𝑣𝑣 < 1 .

During the transition from the divergent phase portrait in Fig 46b ( 𝑓𝑓 = 0.125 ) to the complete isolation phase portrait in Fig 46c ( 𝑓𝑓 = 0 ), a fixed point appears in the lower-right quadrant of the Ap/Bp phase portrait (Fig 46d) once disruptive ecological selection becomes sufficiently strong ( 𝑓𝑓 ≤ 0.1 ). 

Similarly, when hybrid incompatibility is complete (𝑣𝑣 = 0 ), no viable incompatible hybrid offspring are produced. The resulting 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  phase portrait shown in Fig 46e resembles that in Fig 46c. (The asymmetry in the phase portrait arises from a nonzero value of 𝑓𝑓 ,which reduces offspring from inter-niche matings between individuals carrying the same incompatibility allele, and from unequal niche sizes that determine the relative frequency of inter-niche matings.) Because incompatibility alleles diverge across niches, gene flow between niche ecotypes is greatly reduced, as only offspring from inter-niche matings that recreate the parental 𝑃𝑃 or 𝑄𝑄 genotypes can survive. As a result, most offspring arise from intra-niche matings rather

## 42 

than inter-niche matings, allowing the more prevalent incompatibility allele to eliminate the less prevalent allele within each niche. 

During the transition from the divergent 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝 

phase portrait in Fig 46b ( 𝑣𝑣 = 0.3 ) to that in Fig 46e (𝑣𝑣 = 0 ), an intermediate but sufficiently low value of 

𝑣𝑣 (𝑣𝑣 ≤ 0.22 ) produces a fixed point in the lower-right quadrant of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  phase portrait (Fig 46f). 

Together, disruptive ecological selection (parame-ter 𝑓𝑓 ) and hybrid incompatibility selection (parameter 

𝑣𝑣 ) act complementarily to promote fixed-point convergence in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  phase portrait. Weak disruptive ecological selection can be compensated by strong hybrid incompatibility selection, and vice versa. Furthermore, stronger disruptive ecological selection (lower 𝑓𝑓 ) or stronger hybrid incompatibility selection (lower 𝑣𝑣 ) shifts an existing 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed point closer to the lower-right corner of the phase portrait, resulting in stronger postzygotic RI between niche ecotypes. 

The effects of varying niche size ( 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴 ) on system convergence are illustrated in Fig 46g. There appears to be a symmetric range of 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  values around 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  =0.5 that permits the emergence of 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed points (0.3 < 𝑁𝑁 𝐴𝐴  < 0.7 in the Fig 46g example). Outside this range, 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed-point convergence is not observed. 

Next, we used a previously developed GUI pro-gram to investigate how premating barriers mediated by mating biases reinforce postzygotic barriers established through the formation of a stable fixed point in the lower-right quadrant of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  phase portrait. 

Fig 47a shows that, in the absence of premating barriers, a stable fixed point is produced in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝 

phase portrait when 𝑓𝑓 = 0.125 and 𝑣𝑣 = 0.22 .Similarly, Fig 47b shows that, in the absence of hybrid incompatibility, fixed-point convergence in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /

𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  phase portrait—and thus premating RI generated by the mating-bias barrier—occurs when 𝑓𝑓 = 0.125 

and 𝛼𝛼 = 0.3 .

Fig 47c demonstrates that when the postzygotic hybrid-incompatibility barrier shown in Fig 47a is established prior to the premating mating-bias barrier, it facilitates the emergence of the premating barrier via classical reinforcement to reduce ongoing hybrid loss. After both barriers are established, the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  and 

𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed points shift closer to the lower-right corners of their respective phase portraits. This pattern indicates positive reinforcement between the premating mating-bias barrier and the postmating hybrid-incompatibility barrier, strengthening both barriers and increasing overall RI between niche ecotypes. 

In Fig 47d, a weak mating-bias barrier with 𝑓𝑓 =0.125 and 𝛼𝛼 = 0.6 cannot establish fixed-point con-vergence on its own. However, as shown in Fig 47e, by intensifying selection against inter-niche hybrid offspring, the presence of a preexisting postzygotic barrier can facilitate the establishment of such a weak premating barrier via reinforcement. This occurs because, in the mathematical model shown in Fig 3, ecological and sexual selection act complementarily to promote system convergence. Thus, adding intrinsic hybrid inviability to disruptive ecological selection increases inter-niche hybrid loss and lowers the effective value of 𝑓𝑓 , making fixed-point convergence in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  phase portrait more readily attainable. After establishment, reciprocal reinforcement and positive feedback between premating and postzygotic barriers generate strong overall RI. 

We also modified a GUI application originally developed for a two-allele, single-locus habitat-preference model in an open system [48] to investigate how premating barriers mediated by habitat preference reinforce postzygotic barriers formed through the establishment of a fixed point in the lower-right quadrant of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  phase portrait. 

Fig 48a shows that, in the absence of hybrid incom-patibility, fixed-point convergence in the habitat-preference 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 /𝐵𝐵 𝐴𝐴  phase portrait—and thus premating RI generated by habitat preference—occurs when 𝑓𝑓 =0.125 and the habitat-preference parameters are 𝜃𝜃 1 = 

𝜃𝜃 2 = 0.6 .

Fig 48b shows that when the postzygotic hybrid-incompatibility barrier in Fig 47a is established prior to the premating habitat-preference barrier in Fig 48a, it facilitates the emergence of the habitat-preference barrier via reinforcement. After both barriers are established, the 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 /𝐵𝐵 𝐴𝐴  and 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed points shift closer to the lower-right corners of their respective phase portraits. This coordinated shift indicates reciprocal reinforcement between the premating habitat-preference barrier and the postzygotic hybrid-incompatibility barrier, strengthening both barriers and increasing overall RI between niche ecotypes. 

Fig 49a demonstrates that when the postzygotic hybrid-incompatibility barrier in Fig 47a becomes established prior to the emergence of a second, identical postzygotic incompatibility barrier, the fixed points of both barriers ( 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 1/ 𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝 1 and 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 2/ 𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝 2) shift toward the lower-right region of their respective phase portraits. This shift indicates positive reinforcement between the two postzygotic barriers, synergistically strengthening both barriers and increasing overall postmating RI between niche ecotypes.

The example shown in Fig 49b further illustrates that the establishment of an initial postzygotic barrier can facilitate the emergence of a second, weaker postzygotic barrier by increasing the effective strength of disruptive selection against inter-niche hybrid offspring. In this scenario, the second barrier can arise only in the presence of the first barrier, as it is unable to achieve fixed-point convergence independently. Consequently, both barriers become reinforced, leading to stronger overall postmating RI. 

## 2 The effects of having multilocus ecological genotypes and viable ecological hybrids (without CI invasion) on one-locus hybrid-incompatibility barriers 

We modified the GUI application MultiEcoCI to investigate how multilocus ecological genotypes and the presence of viable ecological hybrids influence a one-locus-equivalent hybrid incompatibility model (see Methodology). As expected, increasing the strength of disruptive ecological selection enhances postzygotic hybrid-incompatibility selection, con-sistent with the results shown in Figs 46b–46d. 

As illustrated in Figs 50a–50b, during the pre-CI phase of the simulation (𝑃𝑃 𝑜𝑜 𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃 -𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 𝐺𝐺 = 0 ), viable ecological hybrids weaken disruptive ecological selection and shift the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed point away from the lower-right corner, leading to reduced postzygotic RI. In contrast, in Fig 50c, increasing the number of ecological loci strengthens disruptive ecological selection and shifts the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed point toward the lower-right corner, resulting in stronger postzygotic RI. 

## 3 Invasion of a CI in a two-niche, two-hybrid-incompatibility-allele system with multilocus ecological genotypes 

By setting the number of post-CI generations (𝑃𝑃 𝑜𝑜 𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃 -𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 𝐺𝐺 ) to values greater than zero, we used the modified MultiEcoCI application to examine the invasion dynamics of CI arising in niche 𝐴𝐴 and its effects on system behavior. We present the results in two parts: first, when the CI captures only ecological alleles, and second, when it captures both ecological and hybrid-incompatibility alleles. 

3.1 Invasion of a CI capturing only ecological alleles 

First, we examine the invasion dynamics of a CI that captures only locally adaptive ecological alleles without capturing any hybrid-incompatibility allele. This is implemented by selecting “0” for CI linked allele in the user interface. 

As illustrated by the example in Fig 51, the invasion of a CI capturing only ecological alleles reduces the effective number of ecological gene loci and weakens disruptive ecological selection, leading to either weakened postzygotic RI or system divergence and elimination of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed point. 

3.2 Invasion of a CI capturing both ecological alleles and a hybrid-incompatibility allele 

In contrast, when a CI captures locally adaptive ecological alleles and the locally prevalent hybrid-incompatibility allele in a niche, its invasion anchors the incompatibility allele in the niche where it has a numerical advantage, prevents its introgression into the opposite niche, and further increases its prevalence within the niche. As a result, the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed point shifts to the lower-right corner of the phase portrait. 

Fig 52a shows an example in which invasion of a CI capturing two of three locally adaptive ecological alleles and the prevalent hybrid-incompatibility allele 

𝑃𝑃 in niche 𝐴𝐴 (CI = 𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 0𝑃𝑃 ) shifts the pre-established 

𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed point to the lower-right corner of the phase portrait, resulting in stronger postzygotic RI. Fig 52b shows the same outcome following invasion of a CI capturing one of three ecological alleles and the prevalent hybrid-incompatibility allele (CI = 𝑎𝑎 00 𝑃𝑃 ). Compared with the invasion trajectory in Fig 52a, Fig 52b shows that capturing fewer locally adaptive alleles confers lower invasion fitness, as reflected by the longer generation time required for the CI to reach fixation. In contrast, a CI capturing the less prevalent Q allele ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 0𝑄𝑄 ) in niche 𝐴𝐴 cannot invade and goes extinct. 

## IV. S YSTEMS WITH MULTILOCUS HYBRID -INCOMPATIBILITY GENOTYPES 

We used the GUI application MultiCompCI to examine how direct selection against mismatched hybrid-incompatibility genotypes influences system dynamics in models with multiple incompatibility loci. We then analyzed the invasion dynamics of CI variants capturing different incompatibility alleles. MultiCompCI enables simulations with an arbitrary number of hybrid-incompatibility loci. Following each mating generation, mismatched hybrid offspring were removed using a V-shaped viability function parameterized by the minimum viability value 𝑣𝑣 (see Methodology). To isolate pre-CI dynamics, the post-CI phase was initially disabled by setting the number of post-CI generations (𝑃𝑃 𝑜𝑜 𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃 -𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 𝐺𝐺 ) to zero. In subsequent simulations, the post-CI phase was enabled, and CI mutations capturing different combinations of hybrid-incompatibility alleles were

introduced to evaluate their invasion fitness and effects on overall system behavior. 

## 1 The effects of having multilocus hybrid-incompatibility genotypes (without CI invasion) 

We first examined how varying the number of hybrid-incompatibility gene loci affects population dynamics in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑃𝑃 /𝐵𝐵 𝑃𝑃  phase portrait. To do so, the post-CI phase was disabled by setting 𝑃𝑃 𝑜𝑜 𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃 -𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 𝐺𝐺 = 0 .

Fig 53a shows that, in the model with direct selection against mismatched hybrid offspring using a V-shaped viability function, the 𝐴𝐴 𝑃𝑃 /𝐵𝐵 𝑃𝑃  phase portrait is divergent, even though the same parameter values produce convergence in the one-locus-equivalent model shown in Fig 46d. This difference arises because the elimination functions for mismatched hybrid offspring differ between the two models. 

In the one-locus-equivalent model, elimination of mismatched hybrids is parameterized by 𝑣𝑣 , the total offspring return ratio for the extreme parental genotypes. This measure ( 𝑣𝑣 ) includes both offspring with parental genotypes (i.e., as determined by the actual number of incompatibility gene loci) and parental genotypes regenerated through matings among hybrid offspring. In contrast, in the direct elimination model, incompatible hybrid offspring are removed using a V-shaped viability function based on 

𝑣𝑣 . Although the elimination functions differ slightly and the results do not match exactly, the two models show qualitatively similar trends across parameter changes. 

As demonstrated by the example shown in Figs 53b and 53c, increasing the number of hybrid-incompatibility gene loci promotes system conver-gence and the emergence of fixed points in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑃𝑃 /𝐵𝐵 𝑃𝑃 

phase portrait. This occurs because increasing the number of gene loci increases both hybrid production and hybrid loss, while decreasing the effective value of 𝑣𝑣 . Similarly, as shown in Figs 54a and 54b, directly decreasing 𝑣𝑣 also increases hybrid loss and promotes fixed-point convergence and stronger postzygotic RI. Notably, in Figs 53c and 54b, mismatched hybrid genotypes still exist at the fixed-point genotype distribution, although their frequencies are much lower than those of the parental genotypes. 

In Fig 55, decreasing 𝑓𝑓 increases the strength of ecological selection, such that most offspring are produced from intra-niche rather than inter-niche matings. This shifts the pre-established 𝐴𝐴 𝑃𝑃 /𝐵𝐵 𝑃𝑃  fixed point toward the lower-right corner of the phase portrait, resulting in stronger postzygotic RI. 

In Fig 56, increasing the number of hybrid-incompatibility gene loci strengthens incompatibility selection. This also shifts the pre-established 𝐴𝐴 𝑃𝑃 /𝐵𝐵 𝑃𝑃 

fixed point toward the lower-right corner of the phase portrait. 

## 2 Invasion of a CI capturing hybrid-incompatibility alleles in a two-niche system with multilocus hybrid-incompatibility genotypes 

The invasion dynamics of a CI capturing hybrid-incompatibility alleles in the multilocus mating-bias model are examined by specifying the CI Vector and setting the post-CI generation ( 𝑃𝑃 𝑜𝑜 𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃 -𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 𝐺𝐺 ) to be greater than zero. 

Fig 57a illustrates that, in a model with multiple hybrid-incompatibility gene loci and an established 

𝐴𝐴 𝑃𝑃 /𝐵𝐵 𝑃𝑃  fixed point, a CI that arises within the fixed-point population but captures only a single incompatibility allele ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑝𝑝 000 ) gains no fitness advantage and cannot invade. 

Figs 57b–57d demonstrate that when the CI shown in Fig 57a captures two of the four extreme-𝑃𝑃 

incompatibility alleles ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑝𝑝𝑝𝑝 00 ), it gains a fitness advantage because individuals carrying the CI are less likely to produce mismatched offspring than individuals without the CI. This advantage enables the CI to invade and reach a stable fixed-point polymorphism in the 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 /𝐵𝐵 𝐴𝐴  phase portrait. However, by reducing the effective number of incompatibility gene loci, successful CI invasion shifts the original 

𝐴𝐴 𝑃𝑃 /𝐵𝐵 𝑃𝑃  fixed point away from the lower-right corner of the phase portrait, resulting in weaker postzygotic RI. 

In Figs 57e–57f, when the CI captures three of the four extreme-𝑃𝑃 incompatibility alleles, successful CI invasion reduces the effective number of incompat-ibility gene loci to such an extent that the system becomes divergent, eliminating the fixed points in the 

𝐴𝐴 𝑃𝑃 /𝐵𝐵 𝑃𝑃  phase portrait. 

Fig 58 shows that a CI capturing incompatibility alleles cannot invade when the 𝐴𝐴 𝑃𝑃 /𝐵𝐵 𝑃𝑃  phase portrait lacks a pre-established fixed point. In other words, an adaptive CI mutation capturing a favorable combina-tion of incompatibility alleles cannot invade from near the origin and can only spread within a population already located at a fixed point in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑃𝑃 /𝐵𝐵 𝑃𝑃  phase portrait. 

Fig 59 shows that early CI presence in an initially segregated sympatric population can prevent the later establishment of a fixed point in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑃𝑃 /𝐵𝐵 𝑃𝑃  phase portrait. This can occur when an adaptive CI ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 =

𝑝𝑝𝑝𝑝 0 in the Fig 59 example) that reduces the effective number of incompatibility gene loci is already present when niche ecotypes carrying different incompatibil-ity alleles meet in sympatry, such as during secondary

contact. In this case, CI invasion and spread reduce the effective number of incompatibility loci, thereby inhibiting fixed-point divergence and the evolution of postzygotic RI. This scenario can be simulated in the GUI application by setting 𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃 -𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 𝐺𝐺 to zero and 𝑃𝑃 𝑜𝑜 𝑃𝑃𝑃𝑃 -

𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 𝐺𝐺 to a value greater than one. 

Fig 46a. In a two-niche two-allele model of postzygotic hybrid incompatibility, no system convergence is possible in the absence of disruptive ecological selection. Without disruptive ecological selection (𝑓𝑓 = 0.5 ) and premating barriers (𝛼𝛼 = 1 ), no value of 𝑣𝑣 can create fixed-point convergence in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  phase portrait. In the panmictic population, the more numerous 𝑃𝑃 or 𝑄𝑄 allele eventually eliminates the less numerous, incompatible allele. 

Fig 46b. When the strength of disruptive ecological selection or hybrid incompatibility is weak, the system remains divergent. When 𝑓𝑓 = 0.125 , 𝑣𝑣 = 0.3 , no fixed-point convergence occurs in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  phase portrait. 

Fig 46c. Complete disruptive ecological selection causes the more prevalent hybrid incompatibility allele in each niche to eliminate the less prevalent incompatible allele. 

When disruptive ecological selection is complete ( 𝑓𝑓 = 0 ), inter-niche mating produces no viable offspring, effectively isolating the two niche ecotypes. Under these conditions, the more prevalent incompatibility allele ( 𝑃𝑃 or 𝑄𝑄 ) in each niche eliminates the less frequent incompatible allele. This outcome occurs for all values of 𝑣𝑣 in the range 0 ≤ 𝑣𝑣 < 1 .

Fig 46d. When disruptive ecological selection is sufficiently strong, a fixed point emerges in the lower-right quadrant of the 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨 /𝑩𝑩 𝑨𝑨  phase portrait. In Fig 46b, strengthening disruptive ecological selection by reducing 𝑓𝑓 

to 0.1 produces the fixed point in the lower-right quadrant of the phase portrait. For 𝑣𝑣 = 0.3 , such a fixed point appears for 𝑓𝑓 ≤ 0.1 , marking the transition between system divergence (Fig 46b) and complete isolation (Fig 46c, when 

𝑓𝑓 = 0 ). Lower values of 𝑓𝑓 move the fixed point closer to the lower-right corner.

Fig 47c. Positive feedback between premating mating-bias barriers and postzygotic incompatibility barriers strengthens both barriers and increases overall RI. In the GUI display, the hybrid-incompatibility barrier in Fig 47a first establishes postzygotic RI by generating a stable fixed point at 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  = 0.97617/0.14938. The resulting hybrid loss due to intrinsic incompatibility augments disruptive ecological selection against hybrid offspring from inter-niche matings. Consequently, reinforcement facilitates the emergence of the premating mating-bias barrier shown in Fig 47b. In the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  phase portrait, a mutant 𝑋𝑋 allele arising in niche 𝐴𝐴 successfully invades at the established 

𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed-point population and reaches a stable polymorphic equilibrium with the 𝑌𝑌 allele, producing premating RI. Phase-portrait analyses further show that premating isolation strengthens the postzygotic barrier by shifting its fixed point toward the lower-right corner of the 

𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  phase portrait (from 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  = 0.97617/0.14938 to 

0.99114/0.01626 ), thereby increasing postzygotic RI between niche ecotypes. In turn, the strengthened postzygotic isolation further increases the premating mating-bias barrier via classical reinforcement, shifting the 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁 

fixed point toward the lower-right corner of the Ax/Bx phase portrait (from 𝐴𝐴 𝑁𝑁 /𝐵𝐵 𝑁𝑁  = 0.94002/0.09567 to 0.9902/ 0.01642 ). Together, premating and postzygotic barriers mutually reinforce one another through positive feedback, resulting in stronger overall RI.

Fig 47d. In the absence of hybrid incompatibility barriers, when the parameters 𝒇𝒇 or 𝜶𝜶 are sufficiently high, the system becomes divergent and no stable fixed points occur in the 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨 /𝑩𝑩 𝑨𝑨  phase portrait. Fig 47e. Intrinsic postzygotic RI can add to disruptive ecological selection and intensify hybrid loss from inter-niche matings, thereby facilitating the evolution of premating RI by reinforcement. In the GUI display, the hybrid-incompatibility barrier in Fig 47a first establishes postzygotic RI by generating a stable fixed point at 

𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  = 0.97617/0.14938. The resulting hybrid loss due to intrinsic incompatibility adds to existing disruptive ecological selection against hybrid offspring from inter-niche matings. The increased overall selection against hybrids relaxes the parametric conditions required for a subsequent mating-bias barrier to invade via reinforcement. Consequently, the mating-bias barrier shown in Fig 47d is able to establish system convergence and premating RI to reduce postmating inter-niche hybrid loss, whereas it would not be able to do so on its own. The resulting premating and postmating RI then reinforce one another through positive feedback to produce strong overall RI. 

Fig 48a. In the absence of hybrid incompatibility barri-ers, a stable fixed point appears in the 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨 /𝑩𝑩 𝑨𝑨  phase portrait of a habitat-preference barrier. A GUI applica-tion originally developed for a two-allele, single-locus habitat-preference model in an open system was modified to simulate coupling between habitat-preference and hybrid incompatibility barriers. Parameters 𝜃𝜃 1 and 𝜃𝜃 2 represent the strengths of habitat preference associated with alleles 𝐾𝐾 and 

𝐽𝐽 , respectively. The 𝐾𝐾 allele causes niche-𝐴𝐴 ecotypes carrying 𝐾𝐾 to remain in niche 𝐴𝐴 with probability 1 − 𝜃𝜃 1 and has no effect in niche-𝐵𝐵 ecotypes. Conversely, the 𝐽𝐽 allele causes niche-𝐵𝐵 ecotypes carrying 𝐽𝐽 to remain in niche 𝐵𝐵 with probability 1 − 𝜃𝜃 2 and has no effect in niche-𝐴𝐴 ecotypes. Under the parameter values shown, the 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 /𝐵𝐵 𝐴𝐴  phase portrait converges to a stable equilibrium at 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 /𝐵𝐵 𝐴𝐴  =0.83333/0.16667 .

Fig 49a. Positive feedback between two postzygotic hybrid-incompatibility barriers mutually strengthens each barrier and increases overall postmating RI. The GUI application shown in Fig 47c was modified to model coupling between two distinct postzygotic barriers. In this simulation, both barriers were assigned the same parameter values as in Fig 47a ( 𝑣𝑣 1 = 0.22 for barrier 1; 𝑣𝑣 2 = 0.22 for barrier 2). The first postzygotic barrier (Fig 47a) initially establishes postmating RI by producing a stable fixed point at 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 1/ 𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝 1 = 0.97617/0.14938 . The second hybrid-incompatibility barrier subsequently emerges at this established 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 1/ 𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝 1 fixed point. Phase-portrait analysis shows that the first barrier strengthens the second by shifting its fixed point toward the lower-right region of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 2/ 𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝 2

phase portrait (from 0.97617/0.14938 to 0.99462/ 0.01506 ), thereby increasing postzygotic RI between niche ecotypes. Reciprocally, the strengthened second barrier reinforces the first, shifting its fixed point toward the lower-right region of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 1/ 𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝 1 phase portrait (from 0.97617/ 0.14938 to 0.99462/0.01506 ). Together, the two postzygotic barriers form a positive feedback loop that increases overall postmating RI.

Fig 49b. An existing postzygotic barrier increases the effective strength of selection against inter-niche hybrids, enabling the emergence of a second, weaker postzygotic barrier. Fig 50a. A fixed point appears in the lower-right quadrant of the 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨 /𝑩𝑩 𝑨𝑨  phase portrait for a one-locus-equivalent two-allele model of hybrid incompatibility with three ecological gene loci. In this model, the niche-𝐴𝐴 

and niche-𝐵𝐵 ecotypes are specified by three loci. The habitat resource distribution is set to the Extreme option, in which resources are equally divided (50:50) between the parental ecotypes ( 𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 and 𝑏𝑏𝑏𝑏𝑏𝑏 ), with no resources available to support hybrid ecotypes. Matings between genotypes carrying different incompatibility alleles ( 𝑃𝑃 or 𝑄𝑄 ) produce no viable hybrids, and the strength of incompatibility selection is governed by the viability ratio 𝑣𝑣 . When 𝑣𝑣 is sufficiently low ( 𝑣𝑣 = 0.2 in this example), indicating strong incompatibility selection, an 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed point emerges in the lower-right region of the phase portrait. 

Fig 50b. Viable ecological hybrids weaken disruptive ecological selection, shifting the 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨 /𝑩𝑩 𝑨𝑨  fixed point away from the lower-right corner and shrinking the region of convergence in the 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨 /𝑩𝑩 𝑨𝑨  phase portrait. In Fig 50a, selecting the 90% option for the habitat resource distribution allocates 10% of niche resources to support viable hybrid ecotypes and results in weaker disruptive ecological selection (a higher value of 𝑓𝑓 ). This change shrinks the region of convergence in the lower-right quadrant of the 

𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  phase portrait and shifts the fixed point away from the lower-right corner (from 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  = 0.9566/0.0438 to 

0.9419/0.0581 ), indicating weaker postzygotic RI between niche ecotypes. Selecting the 80% option for habitat distribution eliminates both the region of convergence and the fixed point. 

Fig 50c. Increasing the number of ecological gene loci strengthens disruptive ecological selection and increases postzygotic RI, whereas decreasing the number of ecological gene loci produces the opposite effect. In Fig 50a, increasing the number of ecological gene loci from three to four strengthens disruptive ecological selection (a lower value of 𝑓𝑓 ). This change expands the region of convergence in the lower-right quadrant of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  phase portrait and shifts the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed point closer to the lower-right corner, indicating stronger postzygotic RI between niche ecotypes. Conversely, reducing the number of ecological gene loci from three to two eliminates both the region of convergence and the fixed point.

Fig 51. Invasion of a CI capturing only locally adaptive ecological alleles reduces disruptive ecological selection and weakens postzygotic reproductive isolation. The 

Invasion Point plot shows the successful invasion of a CI capturing two of three locally adaptive ecological alleles in niche 𝐴𝐴 (𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 0) at the fixed point established in Fig 50a. As the CI mutant (initial invasion ratio = 0.01 ) successfully invades and becomes fixed in niche 𝐴𝐴 , it reduces the effective number of ecological gene loci from three to two, thereby weakening disruptive ecological selection. Conse-quently, invasion of the CI eliminates the preestablished fixed-point polymorphism in the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  phase portrait and drives the population vector to the origin, where the 𝑃𝑃 allele is lost. 

Fig 52a. Invasion of a CI that captures two of the three locally adaptive ecological alleles and the locally prevalent incompatibility allele (CI = 𝒂𝒂𝒂𝒂𝒂𝒂 𝑷𝑷 ) shifts the 

𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨 /𝑩𝑩 𝑨𝑨  fixed point to the lower-right corner of the phase portrait. When the CI shown in Fig 51 also captures the locally prevalent incompatibility allele ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 0𝑃𝑃 ), its invasion at the 𝐴𝐴 𝑝𝑝 /𝐵𝐵 𝑝𝑝  fixed point shown in Fig 50a shifts the fixed point to the lower-right corner, resulting in the maximal level of postzygotic RI permitted by 𝑣𝑣 . By anchoring the 𝑃𝑃 allele to the niche-𝐴𝐴 ecotype, the CI prevents introgression of 𝑃𝑃 into niche 𝐵𝐵 , leading to fixation of 𝑃𝑃 in niche 𝐴𝐴 . In contrast, a CI capturing the less prevalent 

𝑄𝑄 allele ( 𝐶𝐶𝐶𝐶 = 𝑎𝑎𝑎𝑎 0𝑄𝑄 ) cannot invade and goes extinct. 

Fig 52b. Invasion of a CI capturing one of the three locally adaptive ecological alleles and the locally prevalent incompatibility allele ( 𝑪𝑪𝑪𝑪 = 𝒂𝒂𝒂𝒂𝒂𝒂 𝑷𝑷 ) shifts the

fixed point established in Fig 50a similarly displaces the fixed point to the lower-right corner of the phase portrait. However, relative to a CI capturing more locally adaptive ecological alleles (Fig 52a), this CI has reduced invasion fitness, as indicated by the longer time required to reach fixation in niche 𝐴𝐴 (~200 generations versus ~100 generations). 

Fig 53a. In a model with three incompatibility gene loci, the 𝑨𝑨 𝑷𝑷 /𝑩𝑩 𝑷𝑷  phase portrait fails to converge without sufficiently strong disruptive ecological selection and hybrid incompatibility selection. Fig 55. In a multilocus incompatibility model, strength-ening disruptive ecological selection (decreasing 𝒇𝒇 )promotes fixed-point convergence in the lower-right quadrant of the 𝑨𝑨 𝑷𝑷 /𝑩𝑩 𝑷𝑷  phase portrait. As shown, a CI capturing one incompatibility allele (CI = 𝑝𝑝 000 ) fails to invade the fixed-point population shown in Fig 56 and goes extinct. Consequently, the post-CI fixed point remains unchanged. 

Fig 57b. In a model with four incompatibility gene loci, successful invasion of a CI capturing two extreme-type incompatibility alleles (CI = 𝑨𝑨𝑨𝑨𝒂𝒂𝒂𝒂 ) leads to weaker postzygotic RI. In contrast to a CI capturing a single incompatibility allele (CI = 𝑝𝑝 000 ; Fig 57a), a CI capturing two extreme-𝑃𝑃 alleles (CI = 𝑝𝑝𝑝𝑝 00 ) gains a fitness advantage and is able to invade. This advantage arises because matings between individuals carrying the CI and individuals with different, mismatched genotypes produce fewer hybrid offspring, which are less viable due to genetic incompatibil-ities. However, because successful invasion of the CI reduces the effective number of incompatibility loci experienced by individuals in the system, postzygotic RI is weakened. This is demonstrated in the Invasion Point plot: as the CI mutant (CI = 𝑝𝑝𝑝𝑝 00 ) invades and reaches a fixed-point polymorphism in the post-CI 𝐴𝐴𝐴𝐴 /𝐵𝐵 𝐴𝐴  phase portrait, the pre-CI 𝐴𝐴 𝑃𝑃 /𝐵𝐵 𝑃𝑃  fixed point shifts away from the lower-right corner of the 𝐴𝐴 𝑃𝑃 /𝐵𝐵 𝑃𝑃  phase portrait, indicating reduced postzygotic RI between niche ecotypes.
